Careful with Manuka honey. It's tasty, but it's a huge scam in a lot of ways. It has a ton of pretty much unsubstantiated health benefits, and as a result a huge industry has sprung up around it. The thing is, it's just the byproduct of bees in New Zealand getting their polled from Manuka trees, which are actually not that great for the bees, like given other sources of pollen, they'll choose Manuka last. So they produce less manuka honey than they would given the same amount of other pollens. As a result, the bees are less healthy and those who make manuka honey kill a lot of the alternative pollen producing plants around their hives. Anyway, this has really become an exploited industry, a pretty large chunk, easily half, of the manuka honey you buy is fake or watered down with syrups or other honey. It's become a huge hassle for smuggling in New Zealand, and it's heavily counterfeited in Asia as part of their alternative medicine market. Basically, be careful to make sure you get the real thing, and also know that you might be contributing to ecosystem damage of plants in New Zealand.

Though if you wanted to flavor your steak as well with the honey, I would say try to taste different honeys, I particular donâ€™t like buckwheat because the smell is a bit weird and it has a bit of an earthy taste but turns sweet later, I just donâ€™t like that kind of taste in my honey.
â€œSome are soft others are hard but all of them are stickyâ€-guga 2021
Just as a little information for future reference, I am a Bee Keeper and produce raw unfiltered as close to Organic as is possible, considering the Bees fly up to 5 kilometres from the Hive. One thing many people don't understand about Commercial Honey products is that well over 30% of shop bought Honey, if not more is diluted with a cheap sugar syrup of some kind, like Corn syrup or Rice syrup, cane syrup and who knows what else. Honey is bought by the Bottlers in 44 gallon drums, these Honeys are blended to make the products found in the stores, the Honey comes from all over the world. Most people don't even know what real fresh Honey actually tastes like, they think what they bought from a local Supermarket is fresh lol. What people need to do is go visit your local Bee Keeper, you will find every area has at least one Honey producer that sells to the public. These local people sell real fresh Honey. Before I started keeping Bees, I thought the stuff I bought from the shop was Honey, I had forgotten what fresh Honey tasted like as I hadn't been on the farm for over 40 years, my Parents had Bees on the farm. I started with one Hive and grew from there, I am by no means a large producer, I am a hobby Bee Keeper more for the environment that to make money. Manuka honey is a great Honey, if you can get true pure Manuka Honey, trouble is that 1000% more Manuka Honey gets sold per year than is produced, this means the majority of honey being sold as Manuka is not or barely has any in it at all. I have two Diabetics in my family, neither could have shop bought Honey as the ones they bought were mixed with a cheap syrup, this syrup is not good for Diabetics, real pure Honey doesn't cause a sugar spike as they found out when I started supplying them with pure fresh raw Honey. Next time if you want to try a proper Honey aged beef, try Honey from your local Bee Keeper as you will be 100% sure it's real Honey and not diluted with anything else ;) There is a great Documentary about the bad habits some of the larger honey Bottlers continue to practice, I won't mention any brands, all you need do is get some real local Honey and then taste the Honey you still have in the Fridge or Cupbord that was bought from the shop to see a huge flavour difference as well as the lack of a funny after taste.

The honey isn't creamy because of enzymes/manuka, it's because it's been creamed. It's like when you whip cream, but with honey instead.  Obviously it's not fluffy like cream, but it goes thicker and some can even be solid at room temp. It's how we prefer to buy our honey in NZ because it's easier to spread on toast, more convenient than a squeezy bottle and also has less crystals.

Yep, honey, yogurt, kiwi, pineapple are all great at tenderizing meat. You know what's even better? Marinating them with a mixture of all of the above (and other seasonings) - and the results are AMAZING (have tried on both chicken and steak). Any of those ingredients works well on they're own, but in combination they're fantastic - you get great results with even a 30 minute marinade, but an overnight marinade on a steak brings results that are UNREAL.  No need to use expensive Manouka honey or let it sit for weeks... and the results are always impressive.  Hell, I didn't believe it when I was told to just mash up a kiwi and spread it on a steak for 20 minutes a side, and was amazed at the delicious results [not a hint of kiwi flavor].    Likewise, one of the best, most flavorful and tender steaks I've ever had was made by a South African friend who marinated it overnight in yogurt and before grilling, coated it in his favorite South African "braai" (bbq) spice blend.  Still trying to replicate those results - the meat was so tender and falling apart it was almost.... shaggy? But not mushy, it was perfection. Can't find the same spice blend online, need to bribe him to bribe his parents to send some extra for me next time they send him a care package, the flavor was indescribable.

The marinade will only go into the meat a couple of millimetres. Waste of time marinading for more than a couple of hours. Don't put salt on the meat and immediately cook it. Salt "brining" - salt draws moisture out of the meat. Leave it for a couple of hours, the moisture will once again go into the meat. Leaving a beautiful crust. BBQ - no flames. Put the meat on the hot griddle over red-hot charcoal. The flames do nothing but make for "impressive" (?) visual effect.Take the meat from the BBQ, wrap in foil, and leave to rest, relax those meat fibres. Honey, epecially manuka honey, is antibacterial, which will not allow the bacteria to develop the complex flavors in the dry-aging process.  Also honey produces hydrogen peroxide, so even if you had vacuum sealed the honey one, you would still have oxidation.  Conclusion: honey should only be used for a marinade, one hour max--not for dry aging.

Dry age a steak in salt crust its traditional islandic way ğŸ˜ but u have to bury it in the ground too! Maybe i gotta explain a little more detailed:  First you have to have a big fat chunk of steak, maybe one of your picanias or so would work. 2nd the salt crust so this is a little tricky basically u want at least (!) a 2-3cm thick salt crust around the meat. So there is hard to explain for me and since this is really ultra traditional from old old time u prob wont find nothing on google (even many islanders dont know it anymore, it is from way back like the before bc time this recipe).  Basically u mix the salt with VERY LITTLE (!!!) fresh source water and apply it onto meat. Then use oven to harden out the crust but dont overdo it, its easy to f this step up and crack the crush etc (in the old times they used the sun to harden the salt crust). Then u take a natural fibre cloth (traditionally linen, jute also works good or cotton but it should be porous enough) and cover it again with a little loose salt, in this you wrap your salt crust meat.  Then most important step! Bury in ground! You should bury at least 50-60cm on the ground also a little bit depending on your meat size. You want at least 40cm of soil over your steak. And the location listen its ok if its rainy sometimes but under no circumstances the water should reach the steak! So choose location wisely too and bury deep enough this will protect from normal rain. And make sure no heavy rain in your location. The soil of course should be free of pesticides and such. Theoretically you could do it in a big container too and buy soil, but make absolutely sure it is 100% organic soil with no fertilizer etc.  So there you leave it for 1-2 months. Best time in year to do is winter, beginning of spring or dry phases of summer. Dont do it in autumn because of the rain and the cold combined its bad conditions (mould etc). Thumb rule is: cold is ok, wet&cold not ok. Hot is ok but not too hot and a little wet is ok but the colder it gets the less wet it can handle. Ok have fun my friend!!! I hope u have success if yes you will be rewarded with one of the most UNIQUE and DELISH dining experience ever i promise!!ğŸ‡®ğŸ‡¸ğŸ‡®ğŸ‡¸ğŸ‡®ğŸ‡¸

Cool idea, but it's not "dry aged" Dry aging meat involves letting a beneficial set of micro organisms grow. The organisms break down the meat fibers and hence make it tender. Honey is a natural antibiotic and does not allow the formation of the useful micro organisms.  So, you were totally relying on the enzymes from the honey. And the smell issues worries me.

Honey actually tends to absorb and attract water, it's a viscous sugary substance that will crystallise if stored incorrectly, though it doesn't go off... Usually. Honey also has antibacterial properties due to certain emzines... Its actually a pretty good on small wounds you just need to cover them well after the fact so nothing else can get in, insects are not your friend in your wound. If you want to dry age with honey, it should be in an environment where not water, not even vapour from another dry age can get to it. It's like salt, it attracts and absorbs water. If you hang a bag of salt in a dry room during the rainy season with the window barley cracked, within a few days water will start to appear in the bag or dripping from it. Just some information...

"Regular honey" may very well be some corn syrup concoction.  Honey is the third most adulterated food in the world.  If you don't know where your honey came from, it might not be honey, or may only be partially honey.  A lot of commercial honey is heating during processing, which can destroy enzymes.  They also filter out the pollen.  When you say, "This honey is delicious", it makes me think that maybe you've never had real honey.
Manuka honey is like natures neosporin, great treatment for burns and cuts.  It tastes sweet and savory, kind of like a tobacco flavor, but is expensive because it is imported from New Zealand.

When I lived in China, the area we lived in didn't really have beef. The 'beef' was actually water buffalo or ox. Usually they were also working animals that died so the meat was _extremely_ tough. We would marinate the meat in papaya juice for  4 hours. For real, good beef I would probably suggest trying 1 hour then seeing how it turns out. It worked wonderfully and gave the meat a surprisingly pleasant fruity aftertaste.

1:28 Non-heated honey is like that. Not just Manuka honey. If the nectar is high in glucose it will always produce crystals which gives the honey this property. Your "regular honey" from walmart is just crappy colored rice/corn syrup. I have tried lots of different honey and Manuka is prolly the most branded honey out there. It's like Appleâ„¢ of honeys. My locally produced clover honey is equal in properties and nutrition value. Even the taste is pretty much the same. Only difference is the color and size of the crystals  :D Don't buy into this BS. Buy honey straight from the local beekeeper and I promise you get the same benefits.   Disclaimer: I am not saying people shouldn't buy Manuka honey because of it's price. If you can afford it then you can of course buy it. I'm just saying people shouldn't spend their money on a well branded product just because they believe it has some miracle properties over others when it actually does not.

Hello. I am only making this a comment for all to see since a few people asked for me to. When it comes to onions, garlic and leeks, absolutely do NOT store these at room temperature in cooking oil. And be very careful while adding garlic, onions and/or leeks to cooking oil even refrigerated.     Raw Garlic, leeks and onions left in cooking oil at room temperature will cause a blood-born illness called Botulism.   Botulism comes across slowly as fly like symptoms while your blood is becoming poisoned.  I almost killed my wife and three children years back because I made seasoned olive oil with fresh rosemary sprigs, raw garlic bulbs and peppercorns. I had no idea that this large bottle of olive oil was becoming toxic. By the time I found out, my family and I was almost dead.   Enjoy recipes using onions, garlic and/or leeks but be very cautious as you cannot tell if botulism has occurred.

After seeing the results I think it may be interesting to take it in a different direction: if the enzymatic activity of honey does exist, why not make a thinned honey bath (like you do with pineapple) soak the meat for a duration to get the enzymes to penetrate the meat, then proceed with the dry-aging process. You will hydrate the meat initially, which may slow the dry age, but if the enzymes remain active during the aging process they should be able to work on the meat and not have the same degree of loss due to spoilage.

Makes sense it didn't taste dry aged.  Honey  is anti-microbial, so while it might have enzymes that break down the proteins, the controlled "rotting" of dry age would actually be hindered with honey.  It's also hydroscopic, so it'll pull water out.  I'd be curious to see a 15 day regular dry aged then 15 day honey "dry aged".
Here's my thought for where to take this next: You get a honey that's not very viscous and has few or dead enzymes, and you dry age it in a tub of it for various amounts of time.  Let me explain.  Honey is well-known for being antimicrobial which is why it can sit unrefrigerated basically indefinitely.  The reason for this is that honey is a supersaturated solution of sugar and water with very little water.  Thus, any microbes (that aren't spores) die in honey because all their internal water gets sucked right out into the honey via osmotic pressure.  See where I'm going?  So, you take some plastic containers about the size of your test pieces of meat (maybe 0.5-1" buffer on each side), you put a spacer on the bottom so the meat isn't sitting on the bottom, put in the meat, and then pour honey into container until it fills all the empty space and with a 1/2" decent layer on top as well.  Then dry age for varying amounts of time.  This accomplishes many things for the experiment.  1) It keeps the honey in contact with the meat at all times; nothing can slide off like in many dry aging experiments. 2) It's an antimicrobial environment surrounding the meat; no mold or bacterial can grow on the meat at all.  3) It lets you use cheap, liquid honey but not an unreasonable amount since you're only filling a relatively small amount of void space. 4) It should _actually_ dry age the meat.  The effect should be similar to the Umai bags: a one-way direction of liquid flow out of the meat.  In this case it's into the honey via osmosis vs. into the refrigerator air via evaporation.  As long as there's enough honey to absorb all the liquid removed (which shouldn't take much but you could calculate it from previous experiments based on how much lighter meat is after dry aging), there should always be an osmotic pressure of water into the honey.  It will continue to literally dry age your meat.  And it's a very controlled environment.  I think taking the enzymes out of the equation helps that.  You could even dry age in corn syrup as a comparison.  Hell, I bet you could leave a steak _OUT_ of the refrigerator for 30 days, completely submerged in honey, and it wouldn't go bad.  Has to be tried, right?
I taste no difference in dry aged vs regular.  I think people act like it is different because they want it to be different.

I'm from an South Asian islandic country named Sri Lanka and our ancestors and our grand parents used to preserve red meat dipped in baskets made with pumpkin and filled with bee honey. They didn't know dry aging back then and they didn't have refrigerators to preserve meat. The only way was to keep the meat dipped in honey. They used to dip beef, pork, stag, boar and deer and those preserved meats taste incredibly good.  Cheers from Sri Lanka.
